    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="f2876148a938448a9c173eb3b6fe3941">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '9b9d6695-3234-4b9a-9ca8-bd78b0d5432a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a target="_blank" class="sm-account__link" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0003-4339-0435"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-orcid"></i><span>orcid.org/0000-0003-4339-0435</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>Institute for Advanced Interdisciplinary Research (iAIR), School of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, University of Jinan, Jinan, 250022 P. R. China</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:ifc_huangm@ujn.edu.cn"><span>ifc_huangm@ujn.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:hongliu@sdu.edu.cn"><span>hongliu@sdu.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:ifc_zhouwj@ujn.edu.cn"><span>ifc_zhouwj@ujn.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zhou/Weijia">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d87345001" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), recognized for their abundant resource availability, are emerging as a viable alternative to conventional batteries. Nevertheless, sluggish electrons/ions kinetics impedes further advancement in SIBs technology. Herein, a novel microcrystalline-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/amorphous-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> (C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>) is developed through in situ low-temperature oxidation of crystalline MoSe<sub>2</sub>. The microcrystalline MoSe<sub>2</sub> acts as a robust framework, while the amorphous MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> phase fills the interstitial spaces. This anode material is characterized by an optimized microcrystalline-amorphous heterointerface. The resultant charge self-regulation effect can be exploited to modulate active electron states, thereby ensuring high-speed and stable sodium storage performance. The heterointerface demonstrates an ultrahigh specific capacity (641.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and maintains splendid rate performances up to 100 A g<sup>−1</sup> (324.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Detailed theoretical and experimental researches indicate that the enhanced performance results from the production of active electronic states, which are initiated by the charge self-regulation effect at the microcrystalline-amorphous heterointerface in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, featuring active Mo─Se bonds, which regulates the interfacial charge redistribution and facilitate electron transfer across the active interface between the microcrystalline and amorphous phases. The findings suggest that the charge self-regulation effect, prompted by the heterointerface network, inherently accelerates electron/ion transport, offering a promising electrode design strategy for fast-charging batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405288-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405288-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) present significant advantages when compared to traditional lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), which is ascribe to affluent sodium resources, low cost, and enhanced safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0001_R_d87344890e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>, <a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0002_R_d87344890e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although potassium ion batteries also show these advantages, the potassium ion radius (1.38 Å) is larger than the sodium ion radius (1.02 Å), resulting in the ion diffusion in the material is blocked, so the development of SIBs is optimal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0003_R_d87344890e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Matter">3</a>-<a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0006_R_d87344890e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the advancement of SIBs has been impeded by the lack of appropriate anode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0007_R_d87344890e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In recent years, the intercalation-type anode materials have limited the development of SIBs due to their low theoretical capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0008_R_d87344890e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Nano">8</a>-<a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0010_R_d87344890e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Nano">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The conversion-type anode materials are represented by transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs), typically represented by the formula MX<sub>2</sub> (where M═Mo, V, W, Re; X═S, Se), have become prospective anode materials for SIBs because of their higher theoretical capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0011_R_d87344890e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0012_R_d87344890e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these, MoSe<sub>2</sub> is notable for its larger layer spacing (≈6.50 Å) and narrow bandgap, which facilitate the insertion and diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0013_R_d87344890e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite these advantages, MoSe<sub>2</sub> as an anode material suffers from significant challenges, including substantial volume expansion and a tendency for easy stacking and structural destruction, leading to considerable capacity decay during cycling. These issues compromise the long-term stability of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In contrast to crystalline MoSe<sub>2</sub>, amorphous MoSe<sub>x</sub> is characterized by short-range order and long-range disorder. This material resembles nanoscale cluster structures with stochastic arrangements at the macroscopic level, featuring isotropic and uniformly distributed active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0014_R_d87344890e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0015_R_d87344890e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The superior performance of amorphous MoSe<sub>x</sub> is connected with its unique coordination and high concentration of unsaturated sites. The presence of unsaturated bonds and higher surface energy further enhance its sodium storage properties. However, challenges such as limited specific surface area and inherent instability remain, posing obstacles to its stability and practical application. It is thus crucial to explore the development of microcrystalline-amorphous nanostructured materials to balance the trade-offs between active sites and stability in anode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0016_R_d87344890e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The construction of microcrystalline-amorphous hybrid materials has shown potential benefits for applications in electrochemical catalysis and energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0017_R_d87344890e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">17</a>, <a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0018_R_d87344890e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, Liu et&nbsp;al. prepared a vanadium oxide material composed of amorphous and crystalline nanodomains through electrodeposition, which formed an efficient heterogeneous interface network within the electrode and demonstrated robust electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0019_R_d87344890e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, Lu et&nbsp;al. synthesized a phosphorus-doped amorphous/nanocrystalline biphase Bi<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>6</sub> structure. As an anode material, its capacity is 300.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, after undergoing 100 cycles at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0020_R_d87344890e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Res.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, crystalline-amorphous biphase materials combine the advantages of both pure crystalline and amorphous phases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0021_R_d87344890e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small Methods">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the crystalline-amorphous heterostructure has been applied in energy storage, the key factors of its role are still vague and need to be further investigated.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we prepared a microcrystalline-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/amorphous-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> (C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>) through in situ low-temperature oxidation of crystalline MoSe<sub>2</sub>. In this configuration, the microcrystalline MoSe<sub>2</sub> regions served as the supporting framework, while the amorphous MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> phase filled the gaps between the microcrystals. The C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> anode demonstrates outstanding electrochemical performances, encompassing remarkable reversible capacity (641.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>), superior cycling stability (332.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and ultrahigh rate performance (324.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 A g<sup>−1</sup>). Moreover, the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>║NVP full batteries attains an exceptional energy density of 352.4&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Experimental and theoretical calculations revealed that the abundant heterointerface network in the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> electrode induced a charge self-regulation effect, causing the center of the d band to move up closer to the Fermi level, activating the Mo─Se bond, further regulating the active electron state on the heterointerface and regulating the interfacial charge redistribution. This effect accelerated electron transfer at the active interface between the microcrystalline and amorphous phases, reduced charge transfer resistance, and enhanced charge carrier mobility and electrochemical activity. This research illustrated that the heterointerface network-induced charge self-regulation effect could intrinsically accelerate electron/ion transport, presenting a robust electrode design paradigm for fast-charging battery applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405288-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405288-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405288-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405288-sec-0030-title">2.1 The Principle of MoSe<sub>2</sub> to Achieve Amorphization</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Oxygen acted as a shear source to sever the Mo─Se bond in MoSe<sub>2</sub>, facilitating the transition from a crystalline to an amorphous phase. This mechanism is analogous to the CO<sub>2</sub>-induced amorphization observed in prior research. Employing oxygen to facilitate amorphization exemplified a classic kinetically controlled process reliant on atomic diffusion, which could be articulated by the following Equation (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202405288-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>):

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202405288-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d314de4e-64d7-43e9-8d0f-d2ce5d6fc250/aenm202405288-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202405288:aenm202405288-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202405288-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi>
                                 <mn>0</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mi>exp</mi>
                              <mfenced close="]" open="[" separators="">
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">−</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>A</mi>
                                       <mn>0</mn>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mi>t</mi>
                                    <mi>exp</mi>
                                    <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mo linebreak="badbreak">−</mo>
                                          <mfrac>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">E</mi>
                                             <mi>RT</mi>
                                          </mfrac>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfenced>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfenced>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\rm{c}} = {{\rm{c}}_0}\exp \left[ { - {A_0}t\exp \left( { - \frac{{\rm{E}}}{{{\rm{RT}}}}} \right)} \right]\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>This equation highlighted that the activation energy (E) and temperature (T) significantly influence the amorphization kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0022_R_d87344890e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Initially, oxygen disrupted the Mo─Se bonds and partially substituted Se atoms in MoSe<sub>2</sub>. The resultant strong electronic polarization effect leads to the formation of Mo-O-Se bonds, which encourage amorphization. As temperature rises, thermally induced atomic rearrangement, facilitated by shear stress, became dominant. The incorporation of oxygen into the MoSe<sub>2</sub> crystal tunnels perturbs the interlayer interactions and can potentially compromise the intralayer bonds, leading to localized lattice deformations or fractures. The accumulation of numerous defects disrupts the crystal symmetry, causing extensive lattice distortions. This shear stress was sufficient to drive atomic rearrangement and lattice collapse, thus facilitating the transition from a crystalline to an amorphous structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0023_R_d87344890e683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synthesis strategy was shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). First, the well-crystallized MoSe<sub>2</sub> nano-flower balls were synthesized through an in situ selenization reaction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Second, C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> was prepared by in situ low-temperature oxidation of crystalline MoSe<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> was a flower-shaped ball structure composed of loose nanosheets. These nanosheets were interwoven to create a large number of voids, which could promote electrolyte penetration and ease volume expansion.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405288-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405288-sec-0040-title">2.2 Theoretical Insight into the Charge Self-Regulation Effect</h3>
            
            <p>The intrinsic electronic properties and their effects on the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behaviors were studied by density functional theory (DFT). Initially, we modeled various crystal faces of MoSe<sub>2</sub>, discovering that these faces exhibited notable oxygen adsorption capacities ((100): −4.9&nbsp;eV; (103): −4.83&nbsp;eV; (110): −4.4&nbsp;eV), as illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0001">1a</a>. Consequently, oxygen readily adsorbed onto MoSe<sub>2</sub>, serving as a shear source and facilitating the transition from a crystalline to an amorphous state. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0001">1a</a> also highlighted differences in the activation of basal plane electronic states between MoSe<sub>2</sub> and the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> heterostructure. The lower d-band center (ε<sub>d</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;−2.72&nbsp;eV) in MoSe<sub>2</sub> contributed to its weaker chemical reactivity with Na<sup>+</sup>. The formation of the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> heterostructure elevated the d-band center (ε<sub>d</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;−2.55&nbsp;eV), bringing it closer to the Fermi level, thus enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption. The heterointerface network of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> induced charge self-regulation effect, so that the electron states of the Mo─d and Se─p orbitals were activated on the Fermi surface, and the activated MoSe bond effectively accelerated electron transfer in the electrochemical processes. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0001">1b,c</a>, the band structure and partial density of states (PDOS) were conducted for both MoSe<sub>2</sub> and C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. MoSe<sub>2</sub> displayed a considerable bandgap of 1.42&nbsp;eV, indicating poor conductivity. In contrast, the heterogeneous interface network in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> activates the electronic states of the Mo─d and Se─p orbitals, inducing charge self-regulation effects to narrow the bandgap, which contributes to increase the considerable number of new gap states on the Fermi surface (0.02&nbsp;eV, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0001">1c</a>), giving the heterostructure metallic properties, which was favorable for the improvement of the conductivity. Introducing O to create a microcrystalline-amorphous heterointerface network activates the Mo─Se bond in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, leading to the self-regulation of the active electronic states and charge engineering at the heterointerface. This process enhanced conductivity and accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0024_R_d87344890e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Energy Chem.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405288-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2bd6e307-726f-4d52-8092-e860ad982b46/aenm202405288-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2bd6e307-726f-4d52-8092-e860ad982b46/aenm202405288-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4fdf6f0b-866d-4937-b654-3a2f6855d2dd/aenm202405288-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2bd6e307-726f-4d52-8092-e860ad982b46/aenm202405288-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405288-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405288" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The adsorption energy of oxygen on the (100), (103), and (110) crystal MoSe<sub>2</sub> surface (left) and structure design of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> from crystalline MoSe<sub>2</sub> and O<sub>2</sub> (right); b,c) The band structure and density of states (PDOS) for MoSe<sub>2</sub> and C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, respectively; d) Average -COHP and the corresponding integral patterns for MoSe<sub>2</sub> and C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>; e) Electron density difference of the microcrystalline-amorphous heterointerface in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. The yellow and blue regions indicate the accumulation of positive and negative charge, respectively; f) Charge density difference maps from the (100) direction in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>; g) HRTEM images of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> heterointerface network.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Crystal Orbital Hamilton Population (COHP) analysis further explored the interaction between the Mo─Se bonds in MoSe<sub>2</sub> and C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0001">1d</a>, the negative and positive values of −COHP correspond to antibonding and bonding states respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0025_R_d87344890e893" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. So.">25</a>, <a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0026_R_d87344890e893" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The absolute integral value of COHP (ICHOP&nbsp;=&nbsp;−1.49&nbsp;eV) for C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> was significantly higher than that for MoSe<sub>2</sub> (ICHOP&nbsp;=&nbsp;−1.44&nbsp;eV). An increase in the absolute value of ICHOP indicated enhanced Mo─Se bond interaction, which activated the Mo─Se bond, thus triggering charge self-regulation effect, regulating the active electrons on the heterointerface, redistributing the interface charge, and facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption and diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0027_R_d87344890e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the charge self-regulation effect induced by the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> heterointerface more readily triggered redox reactions with Na<sup>+</sup>, producing more favorable kinetics and higher electrochemical reversibility. These theoretical predictions substantiated the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance.</p>
            
            <p>To further certificate the charge regulation effect of the active Mo─Se bond on the heterointerface, the charge distribution on the microcrystalline-amorphous heterointerface had been calculated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0001">1e</a>). The analysis of the electron density difference model of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> revealed that the planar charge density along the (100) orientation revealing distinct the interfacial charge redistribution by local charge accumulation and depletion at the heterointerface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0019_R_d87344890e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In consequence, the activated Mo─Se bond caused charge redistribution in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, resulting in extra electrons at the heterointerface, which induced the built-in electric fields and space charge domains, thereby accelerating the Na<sup>+</sup> migration and improving the SIBs’ rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0028_R_d87344890e952" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano-Micro Lett.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>High resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) was used to detect forthe microcrystalline-amorphous heterointerface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0001">1g</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The HRTEM images confirmed that the MoSe<sub>2</sub> microcrystalline region served as a supportive framework, with the amorphous MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> phase filling the interstitial gaps. For the microcrystalline domains, the (103) and (100) crystal faces of MoSe<sub>2</sub> exhibited good crystallizability. The elemental compositions estimated from X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) after etching at 5&nbsp;nm is shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), it could also be observed that the material contains of Mo (18.30 at%), Se (44.55 at%), and O (37.15 at%), so we named the amorphous part of the material as MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) images clearly exhibited diffraction spots for the microcrystalline phase, indicating well-defined crystallinity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Conversely, the absence of diffraction rings for the amorphous MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> suggests a lack of periodic atomic structure, as confirmed by the corresponding HRTEM images. Energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) element mapping displayed a well-defined distribution of Mo, Se, and O elements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405288-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405288-sec-0050-title">2.3 Material Characterizations</h3>
            
            <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> and crystalline MoSe<sub>2</sub> are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0002">2a</a>. The pattern for C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> did not exhibit any characteristic peaks other than those of the substrate, indicating a highly disordered structure. In contrast, the XRD pattern of the precursor could be detected for hexagonal MoSe<sub>2</sub> (PDF#77-1715).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0029_R_d87344890e1028" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Nano">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The chemical states and element composition of MoSe<sub>2</sub> and C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> were determined by XPS studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0030_R_d87344890e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS survey spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) disclosed the existence of Mo 3d, Se 3d, and O 2p peaks. In the high-resolution Mo 3d spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0002">2b</a>), MoSe<sub>2</sub> exhibited two peaks at 228.4 and 232&nbsp;eV, which were ascribed to Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo3d<sub>3/2</sub> of Mo<sup>4+</sup>, respectively. A small amount of Mo<sup>6+</sup> was also detected, likely due to surface oxidation during the testing process. In contrast, C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> primarily exhibited Mo<sup>4+</sup> and Mo<sup>6+</sup> states, with Mo<sup>6+</sup> being predominant, primarily due to the formation of amorphous structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0031_R_d87344890e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a>, <a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0032_R_d87344890e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nano-Micro Lett.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the high-resolution Se 3d spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0002">2c</a>), Se predominantly existed in the form of Se<sup>2−</sup> in MoSe<sub>2</sub>, while in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, Se appeared as both Se<sup>4+</sup> and Se<sup>2−</sup>, indicating the coexistence of microcrystalline MoSe<sub>2</sub> and amorphous MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> within the composite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0033_R_d87344890e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">33</a>, <a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0034_R_d87344890e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Small">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405288-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/492a33b2-5db7-471c-866f-3020dcd0d7d6/aenm202405288-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/492a33b2-5db7-471c-866f-3020dcd0d7d6/aenm202405288-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9fd3e299-f6b2-4d5d-9127-2e178eee4527/aenm202405288-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/492a33b2-5db7-471c-866f-3020dcd0d7d6/aenm202405288-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405288-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405288" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD pattern; b,c) XPS spectra of Mo 3d and Se 3d; d) Normalized Mo K-edge XANES spectra of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, MoSe<sub>2</sub>, and MoO<sub>3</sub>; e) The normalized Mo K-edge EXAFS spectra (circle) of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, MoSe<sub>2</sub>, and MoO<sub>3</sub> as well as the corresponding EXAFS fitting curves (line); f) Normalized Se K-edge XANES spectra of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, SeO<sub>2</sub> and Se foil; g) The normalized Se K-edge EXAFS spectra (circle) of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, SeO<sub>2</sub> and Se foil as well as the corresponding EXAFS fitting curves (line); h) Wavelet transform (WT) contour plots of Mo in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, MoSe<sub>2</sub> and MoO<sub>3</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To comprehend the electronic structure of Mo and Se in MoSe<sub>2</sub> and C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) measurements were conducted.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0035_R_d87344890e1191" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Energy Environ. Sci.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0002">2d</a>, the Mo K-edge XANES spectra for C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> were positioned between those of MoSe<sub>2</sub> and MoO<sub>3</sub> references. This positioning indicated an elevated valence state of Mo, likely due to partial formation of amorphous MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> through in situ oxidation. Similarly, the Se K-edge in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, positioned between Se foil and SeO<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0002">2f</a>), suggested that only a portion of Se underwent oxidation, not achieving a full oxidation to a +4 valence state. The in situ low-temperature oxidation process resulted in partial oxidation, with some MoSe<sub>2</sub> converting to amorphous MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> while the remainder retained its microcrystalline structure. This observation aligned with the HRTEM images. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0002">2e</a> displayed the Fourier-transformed EXAFS (FT-EXAFS) for MoSe<sub>2</sub>, revealing two distinct peaks at 2.16 Å and 2.95 Å in R-space, corresponding to Mo─Se and Mo-Mo bonds, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0011_R_d87344890e1239" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the bond strengths between Mo-Mo and Mo─Se in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> were diminished, suggesting additional coordination involving Mo, which was corresponding to the presence of Mo─O bonds at ≈1 Å. This shorter Mo─O distance in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> suggested enhanced electron transport along the Mo─O pathway. Further analysis was schemed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0002">2g</a>, where the FT-EXAFS for Se in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> exhibited peaks at 1.2 and 2.1 Å in R-space, indicative of Se-O and Se-Se bonds, respectively. These results were corroborated by the wavelet transform (WT) analysis for Mo and Se K-edge EXAFS (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0002">2h</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The WT plot for the Mo K-edge in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> showed a prominent peak at 1 Å, attributed to Mo─O scattering. The altered coordination environment in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> was further validated by the EXAFS spectra in K-space (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The coordination numbers were quantitatively analyzed by fitting the R-space and K-space EXAFS spectra (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). These findings confirmed that C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> comprises a heterogeneous mixture of microcrystalline MoSe<sub>2</sub> and amorphous MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. The presence of defects within this material was further characterized by Electron Spin Resonance (ESR) spectroscopy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> exhibited a strong ESR signal, suggesting a high concentration of defects. These defects, presumably attributable to the amorphous phase induced by in situ low-temperature oxidation, encompassed a significant number of unsaturated atomic bonds, crystallographic dislocations, and various other structural anomalies.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405288-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405288-sec-0060-title">2.4 Electrochemical Performance of the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> Anode</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performances of the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> anode were examined at 0.01–3&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves for the initial three periods at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> were depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0003">3a</a>. Multiple pairs of reduction/oxidation peaks were detected during the discharging/charging cycle, indicative of a conversion reaction. Notably, the initial CV curve differed from subsequent ones, with peaks around 1.98 and 1.45&nbsp;V correlated to the formation of Na<sub>z</sub>MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> through Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation between C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> layers. Additionally, a pronounced reduction peak appeared at about 0.90&nbsp;V, indicating the complete conversion of Na<sub>z</sub>MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> to Mo, Na<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>2</sub>Se. The subsequent oxidation process revealed a distinct peak at 1.5&nbsp;V and a acromion at 1.64&nbsp;V, which was ascribed to the reverse transformation to C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. After the first cycle, the CV profiles aligned closely, demonstrating excellent reversibility and cycle stability. The initial broad reduction peak, spanning 0.01–0.4&nbsp;V, disappeared in later cycles, suggesting an irreversible process likely due to electrolyte disassembly and solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film formation. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0003">3b</a> presented capacity versus voltage (dQ/dV vs V) diagrams across various cycles, showing that the intensity of the redox peaks associated with the reversible insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> remained consistent even after 100 cycles, confirming the high reversibility and structural stability of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. In contrast, significant changes occurred in the redox peaks of the MoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode within 100 cycles. After 10 cycles, the redox peaks gradually changed, forming a sharp redox peak similar to that of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> at the 20th cycle. This change in peak shape could be attributed to a phase transition of the material structure caused by the presence of oxygen in electrolyte, resulting in a reconstruction of the electrode material that gradually made the material more similar to C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. The area enclosed by the dQ/dV curve and the horizontal coordinate represented the amount of capacity added or released by the battery during charging and discharging. The enclosed area in the dQ/dV curve of MoSe<sub>2</sub> progressively increased, indicating an increase in specific capacity, which aligned with the findings in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0003">3c</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0003">3c</a> shows the cycling performance of both C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> and MoSe<sub>2</sub> tested with 1.0&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME&nbsp;=&nbsp;100&nbsp;vol % at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> still maintained a capacity of 641.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> undergo 300 cycles, markedly surpassing the 401.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of the MoSe<sub>2</sub> anode. The initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) for C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> was 75%, which was due to the formation of SEI, corresponded to the first circle of the CV curve.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0036_R_d87344890e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Nano">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With subsequent Coulombic efficiencies (CE) remaining above 97.2% over 300 cycles, underscoring its superior cycling stability. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information) displayed the initial three galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) curves at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information) showed the GCD for the number of cycles, both demonstrating voltage profiles that closely corresponded with the redox behavior demonstrated in the CV and differential capacity (dQ/dV) results. The morphology of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> anode material after different cycles was studied by ex-situ scanning electron microscope (ex-situ SEM) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). The original C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> electrode consisted of carbon black-coated flower-shaped ball. After 10 cycles, it was transformed into a porous structure composed of interconnected nanosphere. After 100 cycles, the interconnected nanosphere become dense. After 300 cycles, the electrodes still maintained a porous structure and were composed of nanosphere. This porous interconnected nanosphere morphology was conducive to relieving mechanical strain stress during long-term cycling. In addition, we also carried out post-mortem SEM images of the electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information), after a long period of charge-discharge cycles, the electrode material undergoes obvious agglomeration and falls off the current collector, causing the electrode material to become inactive and the battery to die. In order to explore the sodium storage performance of the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> anode material in different electrolytes, we selected three types of electrolytes for testing, which are 1.0&nbsp;M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DEC&nbsp;=&nbsp;1:1&nbsp;vol %, 1.0&nbsp;M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in TEGDME&nbsp;=&nbsp;100&nbsp;vol % and 1.0&nbsp;M NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> in Diglyme&nbsp;=&nbsp;100&nbsp;vol %. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information). The performance of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> electrode materials in these electrolytes were severely degraded, while the battery performance was very stable in the electrolyte of 1.0&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME&nbsp;=&nbsp;100 vol%. This was because the weak interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> promoted the de-solvation and storage kinetics of sodium. NaPF<sub>6</sub> had a lower HOMO energy level, which helped to improve oxidation stability, and DME had a higher LOMO level than ester compounds, indicating DME had better reductive stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0037_R_d87344890e1518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">37</a>, <a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0038_R_d87344890e1518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405288-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bf4f5217-f53e-4772-9aa7-e5bfe38b9652/aenm202405288-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bf4f5217-f53e-4772-9aa7-e5bfe38b9652/aenm202405288-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/08e532e3-2734-45e6-abcd-b1e14faf207b/aenm202405288-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bf4f5217-f53e-4772-9aa7-e5bfe38b9652/aenm202405288-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405288-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405288" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> for the first three cycles at scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>; b) dQ/dV plots. Cycling performance at current densities of c) 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and d) 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>; e) Comparison of electrochemical performances among C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> and other reported superior SIBs; f) Rate capability.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The superior long-term cycling ability of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> was assessed at 10 and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>. When the current density was 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles, C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> maintained a significantly specific capacity of 393.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which was prominently higher than MoSe<sub>2</sub> of 235.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, as schemed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> (Supporting Information). Remarkably, C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> sustained a high reversible capacity of 332.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup> after cycling 2000 cycles, demonstrating nearly 100% capacity retention, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0003">3d</a>. The rate capabilities of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> were further evaluated from current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> to 100 A g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0003">3f</a>. When the current density rose to an extremely ultrahigh 100A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity attained 324.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Remarkably, upon reducing the current back to 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity recovered to 589.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating significant reversibility and structural integrity. Even following 450 cycles at an ultrahigh current density, a high capacity of 550.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was sustained, showcasing exceptional rate capability, superior to other anode materials such as Cu<sub>2</sub>SnS<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0039_R_d87344890e1635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ZnS@NC,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0040_R_d87344890e1640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Nano">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0041_R_d87344890e1650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0042_R_d87344890e1660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> P-1T-MoSe<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0013_R_d87344890e1667" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cu<sub>0.54</sub>In<sub>1.15</sub>Se<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0043_R_d87344890e1679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> N-CNS (N-doped 3D mesoporous carbon nanosheets),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0044_R_d87344890e1684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zn-HC,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0045_R_d87344890e1690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sb@C-5,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0046_R_d87344890e1695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Environ. Sci.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In<sub>6</sub>S<sub>7</sub>/ NSC HMS<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0047_R_d87344890e1705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and NPCS(N/P co-doped hard carbon nanospheres) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0003">3e</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0048_R_d87344890e1716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The exceptional cycling stability observed in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> can be attributed to the charge self-regulation effect introduced by the microcrystalline-amorphous heterointerface. This interface modulates the electronic configuration, enhances electron transport kinetics at the junction, and thereby improves both the electrochemical dynamics and reversibility.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405288-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405288-sec-0070-title">2.5 Investigation of Reversible Sodium Storage Mechanism in C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub></h3>
            
            <p>To elucidate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism in the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> anode of SIBs, we performed a comprehensive suite of in situ and ex-situ analyses to investigate its chemical composition and structural evolution during the sodiation/de-sodiation. Initially, the sodium storage mechanism within the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> were investigated by in situ XPS measurements. Throughout this analysis, eleven voltage points were selected to assess the structural transformations occurring within the electrode. The Mo 3d XPS spectrum (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0004">4a</a>) and corresponding mapping image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0004">4b</a>) revealed that at the beginning of the discharging course (1.8&nbsp;V), the prominent peaks of Mo<sup>6+</sup> at 233.1 and 236.3&nbsp;eV, consistent with the 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> orbitals, respectively, shifted slightly toward lower binding energies. This shift was owing primarily to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> between the layers of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, forming Na<sub>z</sub>MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. As discharging continued to 0.8 and 0.4&nbsp;V, the higher valence states of Mo decreased, with peaks indicative of metallic Mo at lower binding energies of 228.5 (Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub>) and 232&nbsp;eV (Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub>) becoming more pronounced, suggesting a partial transformation of Na<sub>z</sub>MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> into metal Mo. In fully discharged state (0.01&nbsp;V), Na<sub>z</sub>MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> was completely reduced to metallic Mo, as only metallic Mo was detected, indicating the complete conversion of Na<sub>z</sub>MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> into Mo, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and Na<sub>2</sub>Se. Accordingly, the Se 3d peak initially at 55.9&nbsp;eV decreased in binding energy as discharge progressed, culminating in the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>Se during discharging through the polyselenide intermediate Na<sub>2</sub>(Se)<sub>n+1</sub> (n &gt;&nbsp;1) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0004">4c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0011_R_d87344890e1827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the subsequent charging course, these reactions reversed, showcasing the high reversibility of the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> electrode, the peaks of Mo 3d and Se 3d returned to their original states at a charging voltage of 3&nbsp;V.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405288-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/be85685c-75c3-460d-af01-a9a788de0e4d/aenm202405288-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/be85685c-75c3-460d-af01-a9a788de0e4d/aenm202405288-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b3890931-932d-4b95-af08-e4cf880140d7/aenm202405288-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/be85685c-75c3-460d-af01-a9a788de0e4d/aenm202405288-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405288-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405288" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XPS spectra for Mo 3d in the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> and b) corresponding mapping image; c) Se 3d in situ XPS spectra; d) In situ XRD patterns for C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> and e,f) corresponding mapping images.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Additionally, in situ XRD analyses were performed to detect the reversible electrochemical reactions of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. Notably, during the initial discharge, peaks indicative of sodium intercalation forming Na<sub>z</sub>MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> were absent, likely due to a high degree of structural disorder and an amorphous state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0004">4d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405288-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405288-bib-0049_R_d87344890e1891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Sci.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon further discharging to 0.8&nbsp;V, diffraction peaks at 22.5° and 37.5°, which corresponding to the (111) and (220) planes of Na<sub>2</sub>Se, were observed. Additionally, the characteristic peaks for Mo appeared at 37.8° and 44.9°, consistented with the (111) and (200) planes, respectively, indicating the conversion of Na<sub>z</sub>MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> to Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>Se (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405288-fig-0004">4e,f</a>). The peak at 44.7°, corresponding to the (200) crystal plane of the collector aluminum foil, was in close proximity to the Mo (111) diffraction peak but still allowed for the observation of changes in Mo peak intensity during discharge. In the charging process, the peaks corresponding to Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Mo gradually diminished, demonstrating the electrochemical reversibility of the C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>.</p>
            
            <p>The reaction mechanism and structural evolution of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> were further obtained through ex-situ transmission electron microscopy (ex-situ TEM) measurements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information). Discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V resulted in the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Mo, confirming the conversion reaction within C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>. The SAED patterns revealed diffraction rings consistent with the (200), (311), and (400) planes of Na<sub>2</sub>Se, and the (200) and (211) planes of Mo. Upon recharging to 3&nbsp;V, the material reverted to its initial state, maintaining a microcrystalline-amorphous heterostructure and thus confirming the excellent reversibility of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process. The reversible stepwise intercalation-conversion reaction process of C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/A-MoSe<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> was obtained by in situ XPS, XRD and ex-situ TEM analysis. The theoretical reaction mechanism is summarized as below:</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Discharge process:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202405288-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray} {\mathrm{Na - ion\;intercalation\;:\;C - MoSe_{2}/A - MoSe_{x}O_{y} + (z)\;Na ^{+}}} \nonumber\\ {{+ e ^{-} \;}} \to {\mathrm{\;Na_{z}MoSe_{x}O_{y}}}\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
